The most common types of host-guest interactions include:\n\n1. Hydrogen Bonding: Many hosts (such as cyclodextrins, calixarenes, and crown ethers) form hydrogen bonds with guest molecules, enabling selective binding and displacement of the indicator.\n\n2. Electrostatic Interactions: Charged hosts (e.g., cucurbiturils, functionalized macrocycles) can interact with oppositely charged guests, providing strong and selective binding.\n\n3. π-π Stacking: Aromatic hosts (like pillararenes or certain calixarenes) can engage in π-π stacking with aromatic guest molecules, which is useful for sensing aromatic analytes.\n\n4. Hydrophobic Interactions: Nonpolar hosts can encapsulate hydrophobic guests, and the displacement of a hydrophobic indicator can be monitored electrochemically.\n\n5. Metal Coordination: Hosts containing metal centers (e.g., metalloporphyrins, metal-organic frameworks) can coordinate with guest ligands, and the competitive binding/displacement of an electroactive indicator can be detected.\n\nIn an IDA-based electrochemical sensor, the host is pre-loaded with an electroactive indicator. Upon introduction of the target analyte (guest), the analyte competes with the indicator for binding to the host. Displacement of the indicator leads to a measurable change in the electrochemical signal, which is used for analyte detection. Hydrogen Bonding: Many hosts (such as cyclodextrins, calixarenes, and crown ethers) form hydrogen bonds with guest molecules, enabling selective binding and displacement of the indicator.\n\n2. Electrostatic Interactions: Charged hosts (e.g., cucurbiturils, functionalized macrocycles) can interact with oppositely charged guests, providing strong and selective binding.\n\n3. π-π Stacking: Aromatic hosts (like pillararenes or certain calixarenes) can engage in π-π stacking with aromatic guest molecules, which is useful for sensing aromatic analytes.\n\n4. Upon introduction of the target analyte (guest), the analyte competes with the indicator for binding to the host. Displacement of the indicator leads to a measurable change in the electrochemical signal, which is used for analyte detection.
